  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f83dd08c-d9fa-41b3-87e4-b4acfe49b3e1'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1851650684128362896">Warsaw under Russian rule in the late 1870s is the setting for Prus’s grand panorama of social conflict, political tension, and personal suffering. The middle-aged hero, Wokulski, successful in business, is being destroyed by his obsessive love for a frigid society <i>doll</i>, Izabela. Embattled aristocrats, the new men of finance, Dickensian tradesmen, and the urban poor all co</span>
  <span id="freeText1851650684128362896" style="display:none">Warsaw under Russian rule in the late 1870s is the setting for Prus’s grand panorama of social conflict, political tension, and personal suffering. The middle-aged hero, Wokulski, successful in business, is being destroyed by his obsessive love for a frigid society <i>doll</i>, Izabela. Embattled aristocrats, the new men of finance, Dickensian tradesmen, and the urban poor all come vividly to life on the vast, superbly detailed canvas against which Wokulski’s personal tragedy is played out.<br /><br />Unlike his Western European counterparts, Prus had to work under official censorship. In this edition, most of the smaller cuts made by the Tsarist censor have been restored, and one longer fragment is included as an appendix.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1851650684128362896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_61077838" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61077838" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61077838?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590173831" href="/book/show/8445416-the-doll">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=i-saw-the-movie-too">i-saw-the-movie-too</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61077838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17809622485823958349"><br/><i> The Doll </i> by Bolesław Prus is undeniably a great masterpiece of Polish realism in literature. This is an epic and detailed tale exploring XIX century Warsaw, tale in which Prus created a vivid picture of the city and people on the background of the economic, ideological and social transformations. <br/><br/>I’ve always loved reading about this nonexistent any more world. I mean that one recorded only on sepia-toned photographs. Men in the tailcoats, women in white gowns, multicultural society, Poles, Jew</span>
  <span id="freeText17809622485823958349" style="display:none"><br><i> The Doll </i> by Bolesław Prus is undeniably a great masterpiece of Polish realism in literature. This is an epic and detailed tale exploring XIX century Warsaw, tale in which Prus created a vivid picture of the city and people on the background of the economic, ideological and social transformations. <br><br>I’ve always loved reading about this nonexistent any more world. I mean that one recorded only on sepia-toned photographs. Men in the tailcoats, women in white gowns, multicultural society, Poles, Jews, Russians, merchants and aristocracy, lost professions … And all that and much more we can find here. From one side it is a story about a strong man who lifted up from the lower classes - literally- how significant is the scene of his exit from the basement, who having attained great wealth - only then considers himself to be worthy of woman from upper echelons. And who is the chosen one? Beautiful, with beauty of porcelain dolls, empty Isabella. <br><br>Wokulski, it's his name, is an incurable romantic. And in this case it’s rather euphemism for bloody damn fool, as ones say: the first sigh of love, the last sigh of reason.<br><br>Prus created a colorful and diverse gallery of minor characters : young scientist Ochocki dreaming about flying machines, old Wokulski’s friend Rzecki yearning for old times, Madame Krzeszowska, baroness from hell and her profligate husband, merry widow Kazia Wąsowska, naughty students sympathizing with socialism and many others. Novel explores their dreams, psychological troubles and involvements in historical events.<br><br>But this is also a story about Warsaw itself, the city of great contrasts, city of poor and rich realistically showed through its streets, parks, shops, buildings and churches. Warsaw, in fact, is one of the characters of the book. With his novel Prus paid a homage to the city and like Lisbon you may visit with Pessoa, Petersburg with Dostoevsky or London with Dickens so Prus became a great chronicler of old Warsaw. <br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1396010115i/9075804._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="17809622485823958349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7004405591530937940">You've probably never heard of this book, but it comes damn close to meeting War and Peace on its own terms.<br/><br/>History rolls forward. The aristocratic scumbags are replaced by capitalist scumbags. The solutions to yesterday's problems become new problems and we don't get anywhere. A great man becomes a great man in pursuit of a vain and hopeless goal that eventually destroys him.   <span id="freeText7004405591530937940" style="display:none">You've probably never heard of this book, but it comes damn close to meeting War and Peace on its own terms.<br /><br />History rolls forward. The aristocratic scumbags are replaced by capitalist scumbags. The solutions to yesterday's problems become new problems and we don't get anywhere. A great man becomes a great man in pursuit of a vain and hopeless goal that eventually destroys him. A world of fops, fools, scoundrels, and nihilists loses something it desperately needs.<br /><br />We can be 99% certain that society is irredeemably fucked. But unless we try to be better, we deprive ourselves of that last 1%.<br /><br />When you hold out hope for human potential, you'll almost definitely be disappointed. But damn it, we've got to hope. And we've got to try.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2233650169" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2233650169" />
    <a title="Czarny Pies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies"><img alt="Czarny Pies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400687408p2/29160274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2233650169?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Czarny Pies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Czarny Pies" href="/user/show/29160274-czarny-pies">Czarny Pies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590173831" href="/book/show/8445416-the-doll">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=polish-lit">polish-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2233650169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4897852020661957399">"The Doll" is as a landmark work of Polish literature that will be of tremendous interest to anyone of Polish descent or having friends who received their education in Poland. It examines all of the major political, cultural and social issues that were debated in Poland in the second half of the nineteenth century. Because The Doll" is taught in Polish secondary schools, the Anglophone reader is assured the pleasure of being able to discuss the work with any of his or her friends in the expatria</span>
  <span id="freeText4897852020661957399" style="display:none">"The Doll" is as a landmark work of Polish literature that will be of tremendous interest to anyone of Polish descent or having friends who received their education in Poland. It examines all of the major political, cultural and social issues that were debated in Poland in the second half of the nineteenth century. Because The Doll" is taught in Polish secondary schools, the Anglophone reader is assured the pleasure of being able to discuss the work with any of his or her friends in the expatriate Polish community. This is a major benefit for a work that poses significant challenges for the Anglophone reader.<br />To properly comprehend "The Doll" one needs a very solid understanding of Polish history, class structure and society in the 19th century. At the same time, the reader needs some experience in reading and decoding works written under censorship in which the author, poet or librettist discusses a political issue using events in other countries as proxies for those in the country that he or she is truly interested in.<br />The key issue is that the events take place in Poland during the 19th century when Poland was partitioned between Russia, Austria and Germany. The hero Stanisław Wokulski has participated in the January Uprising (1863-1864) against Russia and spent a number of years in Siberian exile which indicates that the Polish national question is a major background issue. However, the subject of Polish independence is never directly mentioned.<br />Poles of the Jean Paul II/Solidarity generation view Wokulski as a figure like Julien Sorel from the Red and the Black who makes the perverse decision to become a priest because under the Bourbon Restoration because no other options were available to him. From this perspective Wokulski who has the innate qualities of political leader has no outlet for his natural talents in the Russian occupied Polish Partition. As a consequence he devotes himself entirely to his love for a woman who is inaccessible because she is an aristocrat whereas Wokulski is "works in trade" (i.e. is a retailer).<br />In the eyes of Prus, Wokulski's infatuation is a massive folly in the style of the great Polish romantic poet Adam Mickiewicz. The woman he is obsessed with is both shallow and nasty. Wokulski's loyalist friend Igacy Rzecki is appalled. He wants Wokulski to marry a sensible woman of his own social class and dedicate himself to Polish independence which is referred to by the proxy term "Bonapartism" in order to get past the censor. Rzecki's idea makes no sense either. Wokulski is not living in democracy and because of his revolutionary past would have been too closely monitored by the Tsarist police to participate in any underground organization.<br />Wokulski however is lucid about his situation. He is aware that the best course for him is to concentrate on his business and thus create wealth in Poland. Unfortunately his instincts pull him in the other direction. He is unable to stop loving his fantasy woman. In order to make himself acceptable in her aristocratic milieu he sells his businesses so as to passively live off his investments. When, Wokulski finally sees through the woman that he loves, life loses all meaning for him. His morale collapses and he dies shortly afterwards.<br />"The Doll" contains a lengthy discussion on Poland's Jews. Being a logical positivist, Prus felt that the differences between Jews and Christians were entirely artificial. What Prus desired was for Polish Jews and Catholics to merge into a single progressive community of atheists. Accordingly, he presents the Jews in a very favorable light. Wokulski has many good Jewish friends. Moreover Wokulski vigorously argues that through their business activities which create wealth, the Jews are making an important contribution to Polish society.<br />Prus' sharpest criticisms are for the Polish aristocrats who do nothing to create wealth but simply spend money ostentatiously and amass debts while regarding everyone else with contempt particularly Jews and tradesmen (i.e. retailers.)<br />Prus provides a very insightful portrait of Poland under Russian rule in the second half of the nineteenth century. "The Doll" is in no ways prophetic. Today we know that Poland was finally liberated by "Solidarity" which was in its essence a coalition of the Roman Catholic clergy and blue collar unions. In "The Doll", Prus discounts the Catholic Church as an utterly inconsequential force in Polish society. There is no mention that I can recall either of unions or any working class organizations. "The Doll" is a logical positivist work which assumes that leadership for change in the modern world can only come from the educated middle classes. It is a great novel for any reader willing to forgive it this one blind spot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4897852020661957399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_346935631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/346935631" />
    <a title="John Gaynard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6570207-john-gaynard"><img alt="John Gaynard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442606696p2/6570207.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/346935631?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Gaynard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Gaynard" href="/user/show/6570207-john-gaynard">John Gaynard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer346935631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5643988848373236348">In writing this majestic novel about fin de siècle 19th century Warsaw, Prus illustrated all the social currents that would make Poland such a cauldron of differing identities in the following century. Ostensibly a story about the excruciating infatuation of a successful merchant, Wokulski, for a noble's daughter, the book is also about three generations of men coming to terms with Poland's past and present and trying to break away from the Nobles' Republic in order to create a modern future for</span>
  <span id="freeText5643988848373236348" style="display:none">In writing this majestic novel about fin de siècle 19th century Warsaw, Prus illustrated all the social currents that would make Poland such a cauldron of differing identities in the following century. Ostensibly a story about the excruciating infatuation of a successful merchant, Wokulski, for a noble's daughter, the book is also about three generations of men coming to terms with Poland's past and present and trying to break away from the Nobles' Republic in order to create a modern future for the nation. Although Prus could, naturally, have had no idea of what would happen to his country in the next century, the battle lines of ethno-nationalism, later exploited by the National Democrat anti-Semites, the Catholic Church, the Nazis and the Communists begin to emerge. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5643988848373236348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2226549719" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2226549719" />
    <a title="Richard Newton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19540749-richard-newton"><img alt="Richard Newton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562420591p2/19540749.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2226549719?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Newton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Newton" href="/user/show/19540749-richard-newton">Richard Newton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19540749-richard-newton?shelf=fiction-and-literature">fiction-and-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19540749-richard-newton?shelf=central-asia-to-eastern-europe">central-asia-to-eastern-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19540749-richard-newton?shelf=polish-language-literature-culture">polish-language-literature-culture</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2226549719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15660891641379012444">A book well known and loved in Poland but less well known abroad. This is a shame as I’d have no hesitation in putting it in the same league as Tolstoy - and like Tolstoy this is a huge book, almost 700 pages long. It is helped by a very good translation. <br/><br/>At one level a love story and the power of love to distort someone life, but it is also a story of the relationships between classes - especially between the aristocracy and the self-made man, and it can be read as an existential exploration of</span>
  <span id="freeText15660891641379012444" style="display:none">A book well known and loved in Poland but less well known abroad. This is a shame as I’d have no hesitation in putting it in the same league as Tolstoy - and like Tolstoy this is a huge book, almost 700 pages long. It is helped by a very good translation. <br /><br />At one level a love story and the power of love to distort someone life, but it is also a story of the relationships between classes - especially between the aristocracy and the self-made man, and it can be read as an existential exploration of the meaning of life. On top of this it is an insightful historical drama of Poland under Russian rule in the late 19th century. Finally it is prescient - talking of flying machines, the fall of the aristocracy and future problems for Jews in Europe. <br /><br />So you can see I am a fan. To enjoy it you have to be prepared for 19th century writing - it can’t be accused of ever being spartan writing! Also I found one of voices less convincing than the others. Nevertheless a great novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15660891641379012444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_958344154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/958344154" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5928275-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/958344154?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/5928275-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5928275-howard?shelf=europe">europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer958344154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15723924900998928146">This is ‘the’ acclaimed Polish classic written by Prus serialised in 1897 and full novel form in 1890. It is set in Warshaw in Poland then split between Russian, Austrian and Prussia; the local history preceding the events of the novel appear quite complicated but are detailed to some extent in the book’s notes but uprisings, unification and the elites, politics, modernity etc are the backdrop.<br/><br/>The story is a Dickensian length of 679 pages small font text and a weighty tome – I in fact read the e</span>
  <span id="freeText15723924900998928146" style="display:none">This is ‘the’ acclaimed Polish classic written by Prus serialised in 1897 and full novel form in 1890. It is set in Warshaw in Poland then split between Russian, Austrian and Prussia; the local history preceding the events of the novel appear quite complicated but are detailed to some extent in the book’s notes but uprisings, unification and the elites, politics, modernity etc are the backdrop.<br /><br />The story is a Dickensian length of 679 pages small font text and a weighty tome – I in fact read the ebook version and kept by printed book at home. The style is that classy well constructed literary detailed narrative similar to Eliot, Tolstoy etc. It is not as naturalistic as Zola but given the indications that the Tsar’s censors had a go at it – the book has had the extracted bits put back in (I would have liked the odd note indicating when this happened; and except a whole passage placed in the appendix), it could have been a bit more forthright. Though a character list would have been nice, I didn’t have the same problems I usually do keeping track of ‘Russian-like’ names.<br /><br />The story itself is actually remarkably simple: Wokulski, a now wealthy thirty something shop owner, falls in love with Izabela, an impoverished young noble aristocrat, can his money win her despite his lower status? The length of the tale comes from the intricate detail of the social scene then including Barons, princes, bachelors after inheritances, mistresses and upcoming powerful money men; the histories and development of many of the side characters and importantly the life and perspective of Wokulski’s long standing friend (and shop clerk) Ignacy who has many chapters to himself as first person journal entries. I really enjoyed the style and the clever way Prus has Wokulski acting unselfishly in one way (say paying more for a house than it’s worth to get money to Izabela’s dad) and have her views interpreting this behaviour in exactly the opposite way intended. Travels to Paris, attempted suicides, duels, avenging Baraonesses and adulterous behaviour are all abound. For my interest Izabela is even reading Zola’s 1877 book ‘Une Page d’Amour’.<br /><br />Ignacy is an intriguing character: his concerns for his country, Wokulski, the wider European view (e.g. England’s war in Afghanistan then later with the Zulus, fondness for the Bonapartes, worries for wider war between Russia and Turkey on one side and England, Germany and Austria on the other etc) are wonderful. The emerging science of the day also plays its part (flying machines, Darwinism, metallurgy) on amateur scientist Wolukski.<br /><br />It has to be said there is quite a lot of the possible impacts and prejudices (discussed between characters etc) of the Jewish population, including their power and money, on the modernising Poland e.g. The nobility, in great debt needing dividends, don’t want Wokulski to sell up his business to a Jewish rival (and the clerks don’t want to work for a Jew – except Ignacy who has a more balanced view) – I found it somewhat chilling to think of this backdrop in 1890 and have references to characters taking train journeys to Krakow etc.<br /><br />So in summary: this is a very long read, the story is definitely there (will she, won’t she), the passion and emotion is there (when will he finally realise what she really is) and the history seeps out of every page. The book is a cross between Eliot’s ‘Middlemarch’, Zola’s ‘Money’ and ‘Ladies Delight’ and Tolstoy’s ‘Resurrection’ serialised to be as long as any Dickens. A very worthy read.<br /><br />Some quotes:<br />(Izabela contemplating marriage) ‘Perhaps even… a husband, for people suffer the most terrible misfortunes. But as a lover – that was simply absurd. If necessary, even the most aristocratic of ladies will take a mud bath; but only a madman would enjoy it’ <br /><br />‘She had already almost forgiven him the purchase of the dinner-service and the promissory notes, and the money lost at cards to her father, on which the entire household had lived for several weeks… (no she had not forgiven him that, and never would!)’<br /><br />‘Today’s Hungarians are worth nothing. When the Huns suppressed then in 1849, they protested that every nation has the right to defend its own freedom. But today? They themselves are pushing their way into Bosnia, uninvited, and they call the Bosnians, who are defending themselves, ‘criminals and brigands’.’<br /><br />‘Is it right to equip wretched human hearts with an infinity of yearning, without at least giving them the consolation that death means oblivion?’<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15723924900998928146" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16624456280353481790" style="display:none">One of the best novels written by Polish writers ever. Really, if you are considering which book by Polish writers to read you should choose this one. It isn't a pleasant story. It is one of those that helps one to better understand people and changes in society. One of those that it is really hard to forget.<br /><br />Many levels, many points of views.<br /><br /><b>An amazing study of society and a deep study of human nature.</b><br /><br />This book made me think why (during reading it) I was constantly expecting that Izabela would have fallen in love with Wokulski. Why was I expecting that only because a man loves deeply a woman she must (sooner or later) fells in love too? Truly, it is ridiculous. But so it was (sometimes still is) my blind, romantic vision...</span>

<div id="review_2271548410" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2271548410" />
    <a title="Alec" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60836684-alec"><img alt="Alec" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512350001p2/60836684.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alec" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alec" href="/user/show/60836684-alec">Alec</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2271548410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7576819175363624537">A beautifully written book that provides a heartbreaking and tragic narrative with social context and a broad picture of Polish society in the late 1800s. Prus was able to capture the climate of the time and through his beloved Stas, Ignacy and others, give the reader a clear eyed view into the changing dynamics and hopes of a nation, as well as the driving forces behind its leading members of society. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3627360209" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3627360209" />
    <a title="Dima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65082188-dima"><img alt="Dima" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3627360209?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dima" href="/user/show/65082188-dima">Dima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9785170715961" href="/book/show/25879582">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3627360209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1171959810159888562">All in all - a terrific experience. The Polish Anna Karenina, of sorts. A nuanced and thoughtful criticism of aristocracy, and an ode to entrepreneurship. Also a fascinating character study of people stuck between the two worlds, and the search for meaning. In that sense - quite a bit ahead of its time, highlighting the psychological struggles of a person stuck between two worlds, and what social mobility is possible (or impossible). I also loved the detailed study of the psychology and social-p</span>
  <span id="freeText1171959810159888562" style="display:none">All in all - a terrific experience. The Polish Anna Karenina, of sorts. A nuanced and thoughtful criticism of aristocracy, and an ode to entrepreneurship. Also a fascinating character study of people stuck between the two worlds, and the search for meaning. In that sense - quite a bit ahead of its time, highlighting the psychological struggles of a person stuck between two worlds, and what social mobility is possible (or impossible). I also loved the detailed study of the psychology and social-psychology of the aristocracy. Perhaps one of the better ones I've ever read.<br />I struggled with two topics. First, I found this obsession with "she loves, she loves me not" a bit frustrating at times. It's clear this is a criticism of the societal structure at the time, but it's quite dominating, perhaps too much. It's probably even more dominant here than in Anna Karenina.<br />Finally, I still struggle to reconcile how the Jewish question fits into the story. It's almost like an obsession, with the author oscillating between admiration and violent anti-semitic tropes. It's not really necessary for the main plot's progression. I could buy into the fact that this is social criticism, and is part of the tension in society at that time between valuing capitalism and entrepreneurship on the one hand (and even envying it, to some extent), and hating it, while fearing it, on the other. However, it's still quite harshly done, and is mentioned in nearly every single chapter. I'm still uncertain whether the author is himself an anti-semite (wouldn't surprise me, given the time he wrote the book). <br />Worth a read. A classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1171959810159888562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3545310264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3545310264" />
    <a title="David C Ward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62779504-david-c-ward"><img alt="David C Ward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515367748p2/62779504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3545310264?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David C Ward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David C Ward" href="/user/show/62779504-david-c-ward">David C Ward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12348779-the-doll">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3545310264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11540992532308242559">It’s said there are only two plots: someone comes to town; someone leaves town. In late 19th century Warsaw no one from the upper class/aristocracy leaves and society becomes indolent, backward, and frivolous. The merchant Witulski leaves to make his fortune and to come back to win the beautiful, aristocratic, flighty Bela. A clash of two world views - modern, mercantile, striving vs. reactionary, caste bound, complacent - plays out in Witulski’s obsession. Witulski’s fluctuating state of mind i</span>
  <span id="freeText11540992532308242559" style="display:none">It’s said there are only two plots: someone comes to town; someone leaves town. In late 19th century Warsaw no one from the upper class/aristocracy leaves and society becomes indolent, backward, and frivolous. The merchant Witulski leaves to make his fortune and to come back to win the beautiful, aristocratic, flighty Bela. A clash of two world views - modern, mercantile, striving vs. reactionary, caste bound, complacent - plays out in Witulski’s obsession. Witulski’s fluctuating state of mind is minutely described; off hand, I can’t think of a novel that tracks a man’s romantic feelings in such detail. It’s like Jane Austen in reverse but much longer. Fortunately there are a multitude of lesser characters around the central duo who enliven the narrative. The treatment of the Jewish community and anti-semitism — an antisemitism rooted partially in fear of Jewish energy - is vivid.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11540992532308242559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_323875155" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/323875155" />
    <a title="Dwight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393281-dwight"><img alt="Dwight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314134316p2/1393281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/323875155?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dwight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dwight" href="/user/show/1393281-dwight">Dwight</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer323875155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10087643030166760443"><a href='http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-by-bolesaw-prus.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>My review</a><br/><br/><i>The Doll</i> takes place over an eighteen-month period during 1878-9 and looks at Polish society, with most of the focus on the growing conflict between the upper classes and the emerging tradesmen. Two ideologies are contrasted in the novel—the older Romantic ideal and the newer Positivist outlook. From the <a href='http://www.nybooks.com/blogs/nyrb-news/2011/feb/18/february-classics/' rel='nofollow noopener'>New York Review Books’ page</a>:<blockquote>Prus’s work centers around the stories of three men from three different generations: Wokulski, the fatally flawed and hopelessly love-struck hero; Rzecki, t</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10087643030166760443" style="display:none"><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-by-bolesaw-prus.html" rel="nofollow noopener">My review</a><br /><br /><i>The Doll</i> takes place over an eighteen-month period during 1878-9 and looks at Polish society, with most of the focus on the growing conflict between the upper classes and the emerging tradesmen. Two ideologies are contrasted in the novel—the older Romantic ideal and the newer Positivist outlook. From the <a href="http://www.nybooks.com/blogs/nyrb-news/2011/feb/18/february-classics/" rel="nofollow noopener">New York Review Books’ page</a>:<blockquote>Prus’s work centers around the stories of three men from three different generations: Wokulski, the fatally flawed and hopelessly love-struck hero; Rzecki, the methodical and romantic old clerk; and Ochocki, a bright young scientist who hopes for universal progress in the midst of a darkening political climate. As the stories of the three men intertwine, Prus’s novel spins a web of encounters with an embattled aristocracy, the new men of finance, and the urban poor. Written with a quasi-prophetic sensibility, The Doll looks ahead to the social forces of imperialism, nationalism, and anti-Semitism that would soon hound the entire continent.</blockquote><p>I think the last statement might be a little strong, but they did qualify it with “quasi”—Prus makes it clear that there are no easy solutions to the many problems raised in the novel. Furthermore, he recognizes that advancements, economic or social, come at a cost. As I noted in my first post, the book has its flaws, especially when Prus is a bit heavy-handed on his message, but I still highly recommend it.<br /><br />In the posts listed below I focused on only a few particular characters and a couple of issues in the posts listed below. I hope the excerpts provide a good sample of what you’ll find in the novel, at least as far as style, wit, and humor. I’ll note here that the New York Review Books has the <a href="http://assets.nybooks.com/media/doc/2011/02/15/The-Doll-introduction.pdf" rel="nofollow noopener">introduction</a> to their edition of the book by Stanisław Barańczak online—it provides a good background to the author and the novel.<br /><br />My posts on the book can be found at the links below:<br /><br /><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-by-bolesaw-prus-to-present-our.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Introduction</a><br />Prus intended <i>The Doll</i> "to present our Polish idealists against the background of society’s decay."<br /><br /><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-enchanted-world-of-izabela.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Miss Łęcka</a> and her enchanted world<br /><br /><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-we-may-well-be-witnesses-of.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Stanisław Wokulski</a><br />“So we may well be witnesses of a tragedy"<br /><br /><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-mr-rzecki-is-in-charge-of.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Ignacy Rzecki</a><br />"I can’t leave Warsaw and the store even for a little while."<br /><br /><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-you-cads.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The younger generation</a><br />"You cads!"<br /><br /><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/05/doll-polish-question.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Polish question</a><br />"By wiping out everything that was good, we have produced an artificial selection and protected the worst."</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="10087643030166760443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2738121805" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2738121805" />
    <a title="Rick Rapp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/737599-rick-rapp"><img alt="Rick Rapp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534265385p2/737599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2738121805?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick Rapp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick Rapp" href="/user/show/737599-rick-rapp">Rick Rapp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2738121805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5637543429779845001">Prus has been compared to Chekhov and it's an apt comparison. His lengthy book is ultimately an intimate tale following three unlikely characters: a self-made millionaire longing for acceptance, a hopeless Romantic longing for the days gone by, and an idealistic scientist. The characters are interesting and well-drawn. It's a wonderful insight into Poland in the late 19th century with many disturbing bits of foreshadowing: anti-Semitism, the useless aristocracy, and misguided nationalism. The ch</span>
  <span id="freeText5637543429779845001" style="display:none">Prus has been compared to Chekhov and it's an apt comparison. His lengthy book is ultimately an intimate tale following three unlikely characters: a self-made millionaire longing for acceptance, a hopeless Romantic longing for the days gone by, and an idealistic scientist. The characters are interesting and well-drawn. It's a wonderful insight into Poland in the late 19th century with many disturbing bits of foreshadowing: anti-Semitism, the useless aristocracy, and misguided nationalism. The characters are such as we might find in some circles today. That makes this book particularly timely and disturbing all at once. The biggest conundrum is the title. Calling this opus "The Doll" is like calling Moby Dick "Tattoos": certainly present, but hardly the driving metaphor for the novel. Prus takes aim at all levels of society and at both Christians and Jews. His portrayals are realistic and his dialogue is believable (at least in this translation.) This is a wonderful snapshot of a time in a country about which most people know very little. Yet, humans being what they are, we immediately feel we "know" these characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5637543429779845001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1995254572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1995254572" />
    <a title="Michał/Michael Nikodem/Nicodemus  Hołda/Holda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20937455-micha-michael-nikodem-nicodemus-ho-da-holda"><img alt="Michał/Michael Nikodem/Nicodemus  Hołda/Holda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585272799p2/20937455.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1995254572?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michał/Michael Nikodem/Nicodemus  Hołda/Holda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michał/Michael Nikodem/Nicodemus  Hołda/Holda" href="/user/show/20937455-micha-michael-nikodem-nicodemus-ho-da-holda">Michał/Michael Nikodem/Nicodemus  Hołda/Holda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20937455-micha-michael-nikodem-nicodemus-ho-da-holda?shelf=historical-novels">historical-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1995254572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7072924565093385730">This book is lesson in relationship placed in 19th centaury Warsaw. <br/><br/>It’s about, how we can feel love toward someone, by driving ourselves into feelings based on our imagination from permanent thinking about someone. And yes, we all do this at some level, especially at the beginning of friendship. But I do think that, when you enjoy presence of someone, you want to spend more time with them rather than over process and agonize things that happened only for one. And here attachment to social class</span>
  <span id="freeText7072924565093385730" style="display:none">This book is lesson in relationship placed in 19th centaury Warsaw. <br /><br />It’s about, how we can feel love toward someone, by driving ourselves into feelings based on our imagination from permanent thinking about someone. And yes, we all do this at some level, especially at the beginning of friendship. But I do think that, when you enjoy presence of someone, you want to spend more time with them rather than over process and agonize things that happened only for one. And here attachment to social class also plays his role.<br /><br /> Stanisław Wokulski, owner of shop in Warsaw, has fallen in love with Izabela, beautiful young aristocrat that could not understand life of middle class &amp; middle aged shopkeeper. However with aristocratic descendants. Note that Warsaw at that time was under Russian occupation and wasn't capital city.<br /><br />One day Wokulski has met aristocrat on meeting about investing in goods for economical growth of that region and he took position of coordinator. Just to growth his wealth, without passion though, just as professional.<br />Then he bought horse and wan race giving back all winnings to Izabela, besides that there was scene with one earl that led to duel. With rare shot casted by Stanisław, when bullet hit enemy flintlock part of pistol which breaks tooth of opponent of his.<br /><br />Wokulski spends his money on apartment house and gives money to few friends and customers in need just to make good impression on his sweetheart, he also try to learn some English, as its fashionable language at that time in aristocratic sphere.<br /><br />Moreover Tomasz Łęcki, Izabela's father chained his finances with Stanisław and has become dependent on merchants will. Furthermore Tomasz thinks that this generosity is only matter of friendship.<br /><br />On the other hand, authorized friend of Wokulski, one to get rent, discovers social ties in apartment house. Where some doesn't want to pay at all, and tenant Baroness dislike another tenant, Helena Stawska. When landlord is away in Paris. Guesting at old friend, Suzin, rich Moscow merchant, knowing each other from Siberia. Spending some time there, amazed by it and lunatic for a bit but fortunately he meets a chemist who has invented platinum as lighter metal. But after realizing certain things about relationship he goes by to Poland by impulse reaction given after reading letter from Zasławska, she-chairman of estate.<br /><br />Where he meets her, Kazimiere Wąsowską, widowed lady that is honest about relationships with Wokulski and helps him understand his affections about Izabela, his constant thinking about her. And then Izabela comes over to property. Where at the table Stanisław speaks with her and then there is speech about folk legend of lady with needle in her had that sleeps in underground cave, but there is brave blacksmith that goes to rescues her but fails to meet the quest after attempts to help that poor sleeping creature.<br /><br />Finally Wokulski comes to selling his shop struggling between living for France with Izabella or staying with women that loves him, Kazimiera Wąsowska, that is. Then he travels away from Europe just so his old friends doubt his sanity.<br /><br />There is also aspect of chapters from memories of Ignacy Rzecki, from his diary. Considering Napoleons family (like Napoleona III) and Spring of Nations, particularly Hungarian. Subjects that Poles actively participated. And there is another thing that even without independence Polish people fought for many other.<br /><br />And so, towards ending of the book, in second part there we have court case about titled “doll” that has been suspected to be stolen by looking just like one of dead child. However after explanation and proofs, the case is dismissed. And another case with students that aren’t paying for rent to new owner, baroness.<br /><br />This epos describes well scenery of life in that time and place, still is not pure copy of real events however it’s highly probable. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7072924565093385730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XmtYTwVQwib7Wf8Hw71oSFZJ6ewm8dhUU0QvsCXqBlbsUxQjCio03Ba+vWoTBM0uO9FceX2QKii1Y8+4jZDz8w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

This division underlines the relationship of human life with <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18041515253175016337\" style=\"display:none\">Each of the four parts represents a season in the life of the peasants — Autumn (published in 1904), Winter (published in 1904), Spring (published in 1906), and Summer (published in 1909). This division underlines the relationship of human life with nature.\n\nReymont decided to write about peasant life because of historical events, which took place in Polish villages in the early 20th century. The fact that the author was brought up in a village has a significant influence on the novel’s plot.\n\nIn Chłopi, Reymont created a more complete and suggestive picture of country life than any other Polish writer. The novel impresses the reader with its authenticity of the material reality, customs, behaviour and spiritual culture of the people. The composition of the novel astonishes the reader with its strict simplicity and functionality. The titles of the various volumes signal a tetralogy in one vegetational cycle, which regulates the eternal and repeatable rhythm of village life. Parallel to that rhythm is a calendar of religion and customs, also repeatable. In such boundaries Reymont placed a colourful country community with sharply drawn individual portraits. The repertoire of human experience and the richness of spiritual life, which can be compared with the repertoire of Biblical books and Greek myths, has no doctrinal ideas or didactic exemplifications. The author of Chłopi does not believe in doctrines, but rather in his own knowledge of life, the mentality of the people described, and his sense of reality.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/297006.Boles_aw_Prus">About Bolesław Prus</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/297006.Boles_aw_Prus"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1210605462p3/297006.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/297006.Boles_aw_Prus">Bolesław Prus

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8985129609079232370">Bolesław Prus (pronounced:[bɔ'lεswaf 'prus]; Hrubieszów, August 20, 1847 – May 19, 1912, Warsaw), whose actual name was Aleksander Głowacki, was a Polish journalist and novelist who is known especially for his novels The Doll and Pharaoh. He was the leading representative of realism in 19th-century Polish literature and remains a distinctive voice in world literature. Głowacki took the pen name "P</span>
  <span id="freeText8985129609079232370" style="display:none">Bolesław Prus (pronounced:[bɔ'lεswaf 'prus]; Hrubieszów, August 20, 1847 – May 19, 1912, Warsaw), whose actual name was Aleksander Głowacki, was a Polish journalist and novelist who is known especially for his novels The Doll and Pharaoh. He was the leading representative of realism in 19th-century Polish literature and remains a distinctive voice in world literature. Głowacki took the pen name "Prus" from the name of his family coat-of-arms.<br /><br />An indelible mark was left on Prus by his experiences as a 15-year-old soldier in the Polish 1863 Uprising against Imperial Russia, in which he suffered severe injuries and imprisonment.<br /><br />In 1872 at age 25, in Warsaw, Prus settled into a distinguished 40-year journalistic career. As a sideline, to augment his income and to appeal to readers through their aesthetic sensibilities, he began writing short stories. Achieving success with these, he went on to employ a broader canvas; between 1886 and 1895, he completed four major novels on "great questions of our age."<br /><br />Of his novels, perennial favorites with readers are The Doll and Pharaoh. The Doll describes the romantic infatuation of a man of action who is frustrated by the backwardness of his society. Pharaoh, Prus' only historical novel, is a study of political power and statecraft, set in ancient Egypt at the fall of its 20th Dynasty and of the New Kingdom.<br /><br />Wikipedia</span>
  <a data-text-id="8985129609079232370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
